reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, output_size, dropout=0.0):
        """
        Initialize the LSTM model.

        :param input_size: The number of expected features in the input `x`
        :param hidden_size: The number of features in the hidden state `h`
        :param num_layers: Number of recurrent layers
        :param output_size: The number of output features
        :param dropout: If non-zero, introduces a Dropout layer on the outputs of each LSTM layer except the last layer, with dropout probability equal to `dropout`
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        # Initialize hidden state with random values
        self.lstm = nn.LSTM(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, batch_first=True, dropout=dropout, bidirectional=False)
        self.fc = nn.Linear(hidden_size, output_size)
    
    def forward(self, x,h0,c0):
        """
        Forward pass through the LSTM model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, sequence_length, input_size)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, sequence_length, output_size)
        """
        
        # Forward propagate LSTM
        out, state = self.lstm(x, (h0, c0))  # out: tensor of shape (batch_size, seq_length, hidden_size)
        
        # Decode the hidden state of the last time step
        out = self.fc(out[:, -1, :])  # out: tensor of shape (batch_size, output_size)
        
        return state[0]

# Test code
batch_size = 10
sequence_length = 512
input_size = 128
hidden_size = 256
num_layers = 6
output_size = 10
dropout = 0.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, sequence_length, input_size),torch.rand((num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size)),torch.rand((num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size))]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, output_size, dropout]

But writing a 6-layer LSTM from scratch in CUDA is quite involved. Each layer has 4 weight matrices (Wi, Wf, Wg, Wo for input and Ui, Uf, Ug, Uo for hidden). Actually PyTorch's LSTM uses `weight_ih_l[k]` and `weight_hh_l[k]`, each of shape `(4*hidden_size, input_size_or_hidden_size)`. So for layer 0: W_ih is (1024, 128), W_hh is (1024, 256). The recurrence is: i_t = sigmoid(W_ii x_t + b_ii + W_hi h_{t-1} + b_hi) f_t = sigmoid(W_if x_t + b_if + W_hf h_{t-1} + b_hf) g_t = tanh(W_ig x_t + b_ig + W_hg h_{t-1} + b_hg) o_t = sigmoid(W_io x_t + b_io + W_ho h_{t-1} + b_ho) c_t = f_t * c_{t-1} + i_t * g_t h_t = o_t * tanh(c_t) PyTorch's LSTM uses one big matrix multiply per timestep per layer: [x_t, h_{t-1}] @ W^T + b, where W is shaped appropriately. But our approach does separate GEMMs. The input GEMM for layer 0 is a single cublas call: C = A @ B where A is (5120, 128), B is (128, 1024). That's a small GEMM. Let's think about kernel design: For a single layer: - Input: `inp` shape (B, S, I) - W_ih: (4H, I), W_hh: (4H, H), b: (4H,) - h0: (B, H), c0: (B, H) We first compute `inp_proj = inp @ W_ih.T + b` using cuBLAS. (B*S, 4H) Then we launch a kernel: - Grid: B blocks (one per batch element) - Each block processes one sequence of length S. - Each block loads W_hh into shared memory. W_hh is (4H, H) = 1024 * 256 = 262K floats = 1MB. That's a bit large for shared memory. - Actually we can tile it. Or we can keep W_hh in registers? No. - Alternatively, we don't load all of W_hh into shared memory. We compute `h @ W_hh.T` on the fly using threads. Each thread could compute one element of the gates. For a given h_prev (H elements), and W_hh (4H x H), we want gates (4H elements). Number of threads per block: maybe 4H = 1024 threads. Each thread computes one row of W_hh dot h_prev. That means each thread loads all of h_prev (256 floats) and one row of W_hh (256 floats). So we can keep h_prev in shared memory (256*4=1KB), and read W_hh from global memory. 1024 threads * 256 floats = reading 256K floats from global memory per timestep. Over 512 timesteps that's 131M floats = 524 MB of reads. That's a lot, but maybe okay if cached in L2. W_hh is 1MB, so it can fit in L2 cache. Actually, W_hh is (1024, 256). Each thread reads one row: 256 floats. The whole W_hh is read by the block each timestep? No, each block handles one batch element. B=10, so there are 10 blocks. But W_hh could stay in L2 if we're lucky. L2 cache on A100 is 40MB, so 1MB fits easily. So it should be cached well. Wait, but there are 10 blocks, potentially running concurrently on different SMs. Total W_hh size is 1MB. All blocks read the same W_hh. It will be in L2, so it's fast. Similarly, h_prev is different per batch but small. inp_proj is (B, S, 4H). Each block reads its sequence element: S * 4H floats = 512 * 1024 = 524K floats per block. Total reads: 5.2M floats = 20MB. This is also fine. So kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void lstm_layer_kernel( const float* __restrict__ inp_proj, // (B, S, 4H) const float* __restrict__ W_hh, // (4H, H) const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ h_out, // (S, B, H) or (B, S, H) - output h for all timesteps? But for next layer we need all timesteps. float* __restrict__ h_final, // (B, H) float* __restrict__ c_final, // (B, H) int B, int S, int H) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; // Each block handles one batch element // Use 4H threads (or some multiple) const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int tid4 = tid % (4*H); // or just tid if blockDim.x = 4*H // Load h and c into shared memory / registers __shared__ float hShared[256]; // H __shared__ float cShared[256]; __shared__ float gates[1024]; // 4*H // Initialize h and c from h0, c0 if (tid < H) { hShared[tid] = h0[b * H + tid]; cShared[tid] = c0[b * H + tid]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < S; ++t) { // Compute h @ W_hh.T for this gate // tid in [0, 4H) float gate_val = 0.0f; // Each thread computes dot(hShared, W_hh[tid, :]) for (int i = 0; i < H; ++i) { gate_val += hShared[i] * W_hh[tid * H + i]; // W_hh is row-major (4H, H) } // Add input projection gate_val += inp_proj[((b * S) + t) * (4*H) + tid]; gates[tid] = gate_val; __syncthreads(); // Now compute i, f, g, o if (tid < H) { float i_gate = sigmoid(gates[tid]); float f_gate = sigmoid(gates[tid + H]); float g_gate = tanh(gates[tid + 2*H]); float o_gate = sigmoid(gates[tid + 3*H]); cShared[tid] = f_gate * cShared[tid] + i_gate * g_gate; hShared[tid] = o_gate * tanh(cShared[tid]); } __syncthreads(); // Write output h for this timestep if needed // For next layer, we need the full sequence output if (tid < H) { h_out[((b * S) + t) * H + tid] = hShared[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } // Write final h and c if (tid < H) { h_final[b * H + tid] = hShared[tid]; c_final[b * H + tid] = cShared[tid]; } } ``` Wait, but `tid` ranges up to maybe 1024. `if (tid < H)` will be true for the first 256 threads. The other threads just participate in computing the gates. This is simple and might work. But we need to check performance. - Block size: 1024 threads - Grid size: 10 blocks (batch size) - Each block iterates 512 timesteps - At each timestep, each thread does H=256 multiply-adds in a loop. That's 256 FMAs per thread per timestep. - Total FMAs per block per timestep: 1024 * 256 = 262K. - Total FMAs for one layer: 10 * 512 * 262K = 1.34B FMAs. - At 19.5 TFLOPS for fp32 on A100, that's 0.069 ms. But memory bandwidth and loop overhead will increase it. Wait, but there are 6 layers, so multiply by 6: ~8B FMAs = 0.4 ms? That seems too optimistic because the threads are doing scalar loops. Actually, this kernel has every thread doing a dot product in a scalar loop. This is very inefficient because: 1. Memory access to W_hh is strided: thread tid reads W_hh[tid * H + i] as i increments. So consecutive threads read elements separated by H floats = 1KB. That's bad for memory coalescing. 2. The dot product is not vectorized. We should improve the W_hh multiplication. Instead of each thread computing a full row, we can have threads collaborate. Better approach: Transpose W_hh so that it's (H, 4H). Then for computing `h @ W_hh^T`, we want `gates = W_hh @ h` if W_hh is (4H, H). Actually if W_hh is stored as (H, 4H) with the transpose, then `gates[j] = sum_i h[i] * W_hh[i * 4H + j]`. Then consecutive threads (tid) can read consecutive elements from `W_hh[i * 4H + tid]`, which is coalesced! Yes! If we store `W_hh_t` as shape (H, 4H) where `W_hh_t[i, j] = W_hh[j, i]`, then: `gates[tid] = sum_i h[i] * W_hh_t[i * 4H + tid]` Now all threads read `W_hh_t[i * 4H + tid]` which is coalesced. But wait, if blockDim.x = 4H = 1024, then the first 1024 threads each compute one gate. But summing over H requires a reduction per thread. Each thread still does H loads from W_hh_t, but they are coalesced within each i iteration. And h[i] is broadcast from shared memory. Actually, even better: we can use 256 threads (one per h element) and have them compute partial products, then reduce? No, that doesn't map well. Let's stick with 1024 threads per block, one per gate. Each thread computes one dot product: ``` float sum = 0; for (int i = 0; i < H; ++i) { sum += h[i] * W_hh_t[i * 4H + tid]; } ``` Since h[i] is in shared memory (broadcast), and `W_hh_t[i*4H + tid]` is coalesced across threads (tid is the thread index). This is much better. But each thread does 256 iterations. The loop overhead might be okay. We can unroll it since H=256 is known at compile time? But H is a runtime parameter for the kernel. We can template it. Actually, hidden_size is given in the constructor. It's 256. We can compile with `H=256` as a template parameter. But `load_inline` makes that a bit tricky. So the plan is: 1. `ModelNew` has `self.lstm` and `self.fc` (for weight compatibility) 2. In `forward(x, h0, c0)`: a. Extract weights/biases from `self.lstm` for each layer b. `h = h0`, `c = c0` c. `inp = x` d. For each layer `l`: - `W_ih, W_hh, b_ih, b_hh = self.lstm.weight_ih_l[l], ...` - Compute `inp_proj = inp @ W_ih.T + b_ih + b_hh` [shape (B, S, 4H)] - Call custom kernel with `inp_proj, W_hh, h[l], c[l]` - Kernel outputs: `out` (B, S, H) - the h sequence for all timesteps, and `h_final` (B, H), `c_final` (B, H) - `inp = out` (for next layer) - Store `h_final` in `h_n[l]`, `c_final` in `c_n[l]` e. Return `h_n` (shape (L, B, H)) Wait, `h0` and `c0` have shape `(num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size)`. We need to pass `h0[l]` to layer l. For layer 0, the input `x` has shape `(B, S, I)`. For layers 1+, input has shape `(B, S, H)`. The kernel will take: - `inp_proj`: (B, S, 4H) - `W_hh`: (4H, H) transposed to (H, 4H) on CPU side - `h0_layer`: (B, H) - `c0_layer`: (B, H) - `h_out`: (B, S, H) - `h_final`: (B, H) - `c_final`: (B, H) Kernel launch: `B` blocks (one per batch element), `4*H` threads per block. Inside kernel: ```cuda __global__ void lstm_recurrent_kernel( const float* __restrict__ inp_proj, // (B, S, 4H) const float* __restrict__ W_hh_t, // (H, 4H) transposed const float* __restrict__ h0, const float* __restrict__ c0, float* __restrict__ h_out, // (B, S, H) float* __restrict__ h_final, float* __restrict__ c_final, int B, int S, int H, int gate_stride) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int total_gates = 4 * H; if (b >= B || tid >= total_gates) return; extern __shared__ float shared[]; float* h_shared = shared; // H float* c_shared = shared + H; // H // Could also put gates in shared, but we can compute directly // Initialize if (tid < H) { h_shared[tid] = h0[b * H + tid]; c_shared[tid] = c0[b * H + tid]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < S; ++t) { float gate_val = 0.0f; // Compute dot(h_shared, W_hh[:, tid]) // W_hh_t is (H, 4H), row-major for (int i = 0; i < H; ++i) { gate_val += h_shared[i] * W_hh_t[i * total_gates + tid]; } gate_val += inp_proj[((b * S) + t) * total_gates + tid]; // We need all gates computed before updating h and c // But only the first H threads need to do the cell update // To avoid divergence, we could store gates in shared memory // Let's stores in shared memory if (tid < total_gates) { shared[2*H + tid] = gate_val; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < H) { float i_gate = sigmoid(shared[2*H + tid]); float f_gate = sigmoid(shared[2*H + tid + H]); float g_gate = tanhf(shared[2*H + tid + 2*H]); float o_gate = sigmoid(shared[2*H + tid + 3*H]); c_shared[tid] = f_gate * c_shared[tid] + i_gate * g_gate; h_shared[tid] = o_gate * tanhf(c_shared[tid]); } __syncthreads(); if (tid < H) { h_out[((b * S) + t) * H + tid] = h_shared[tid]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid < H) { h_final[b * H + tid] = h_shared[tid]; c_final[b * H + tid] = c_shared[tid]; } } ``` Shared memory size needed: 2*H + 4*H = 6*H floats. For H=256, that's 1536 floats = 6KB. Very small. Wait, but I used `extern __shared__ float shared[];` and passed the size at launch. With `blockDim.x = 4*H`, and shared memory for 6*H floats. So if we make each thread compute 4 gates (gate indices tid*4, tid*4+1, tid*4+2, tid*4+3), then thread tid reads: `W_hh_t[i * 4H + tid*4 + 0..3]` as a float4. But then thread tid computes 4 dot products, not 1. It computes gate_val[0..3] = sum_i h[i] * W_hh_t[i*4H + tid*4 + j] for j=0..3. With float4 loads, each thread gets 4 W values per i. Then each thread updates 4 sums. This reduces the loop iterations from 256 to 64 (since we do 4 at a time). And uses vector loads. This is much better! Let's work this out: - Block size: 256 threads (since 1024 gates / 4 gates per thread = 256 threads) - Each thread handles gate indices `tid*4 + j` for j=0,1,2,3. - BlockDim.x = 256. - Total blocks = B = 10. - In the loop over i from 0 to H-1 in steps of 4: - Load `h_vec = make_float4(h_shared[i], h_shared[i+1], h_shared[i+2], h_shared[i+3])`? Wait, h_shared[i+0..3] are 4 different h values. We want to multiply each h value with 4 different W values? No. Wait, let's carefully write: `gate_g = sum_{i=0}^{H-1} h[i] * W_hh_t[i, g]` For thread tid, `g = tid*4 + j` for j in [0,3]: `gate_g = sum_{i=0}^{H-1} h[i] * W_hh_t[i, g]` To vectorize over `i`, we process `i` in chunks of 4. For chunk `i0`, we need: `h[i0+0..3]` and `W_hh_t[i0+0..3, g]` for each g. If we load `W_hh_t` as: `W0 = *(float4*)(W_hh_t + (i0+0)*4H + g0)` where g0 = tid*4 `W1 = *(float4*)(W_hh_t + (i0+1)*4H + g0)` `W2 = *(float4*)(W_hh_t + (i0+2)*4H + g0)` `W3 = *(float4*)(W_hh_t + (i0+3)*4H + g0)` Wait, `W_hh_t[(i0+k)*4H + g0]` is a float4 starting at column g0, row i0+k. That loads 4 consecutive columns. Since g0 = tid*4, and threads in a warp have tid=0..31, they load columns 0..3, 4..7, 8..11, etc. These are consecutive 16-byte chunks (64 bytes total per warp), fully coalesced. But then how do we compute the dot product? For each j (0..3) corresponding to gate g0+j: `gate_g0_j += h[i0+0] * W0.wj + h[i0+1] * W1.wj + h[i0+2] * W2.wj + h[i0+3] * W3.wj` where wj is element j of the float4. So we can do: ``` float4 h = make_float4(h_shared[i], h_shared[i+1], h_shared[i+2], h_shared[i+3]); float4 w0 = load(i0+0, g0); float4 w1 = load(i0+1, g0); float4 w2 = load(i0+2, g0); float4 w3 = load(i0+3, g0); acc0 += h.x * w0.x + h.y * w1.x + h.z * w2.x + h.w * w3.x; acc1 += h.x * w0.y + h.y * w1.y + h.z * w2.y + h.w * w3.y; acc2 += h.x * w0.z + h.y * w1.z + h.z * w2.z + h.w * w3.z; acc3 += h.x * w0.w + h.y * w1.w + h.z * w2.w + h.w * w3.w; ``` Each iteration does 16 FMAs. Since we loop H/4 = 64 times, each thread does 1024 FMAs per timestep. With 256 threads per block, total FMAs per block per timestep = 1024 * 256 = 262K. That's the same total FMAs, but fewer loop iterations and vectorized loads. This should be much faster. Actually, 1024 FMAs per thread per timestep. 512 timesteps = 524K FMAs per thread. At 1 FMA per clock per thread (with enough warps), and 256 threads per block = 8 warps. 8 warps might not fully hide latency (need ~16 warps for FP32 on A100). So throughput might be ~0.5 FMA per clock. 524K FMAs / 0.5 = 1M clocks. At 1.41 GHz, that's 0.7 ms per block? Wait, per block? No, per thread. A100 SM executes warps, not threads. So 8 warps per SM. Each warp has 32 threads. Each warp does 32 FMAs per instruction? Actually a warp executes the same instruction on 32 threads, so one FMA instruction per warp per clock. 8 warps -> 8 FMA instructions per clock -> 8*32=256 FMAs per clock per SM? No, each FMA instruction is for 32 threads in the warp. An SM has 64 FP32 cores, so it can execute 2 warps worth of FMAs per clock. So 2 FMA instructions per clock per SM (each handling one warp). If there are 8 warps, they can be interleaved. Ideal throughput for FMA is 1 per thread per 2 clocks (because 64 cores, 32 threads per warp, so 2 warps per clock). So each thread does one FMA per 2 clocks. So 524K FMAs per thread * 2 clocks = 1.05M clocks. But there are 256 threads per block. A block gets one SM. The SM processes 256 threads = 8 warps. To make this somewhat efficient, I'll have each thread block handle one layer, and within the block, threads will compute gates for all batch elements. Here's my plan for the kernel: - One kernel launch per layer - The kernel takes x (input sequence for this layer), h0, c0, W_ih, W_hh, b_ih, b_hh - It loops over timesteps - At each timestep, it computes gates for all batch elements - It updates h and c - After the loop, it writes h and c back - It also writes the output sequence if it's not the last layer OR if we need it Wait, for all layers except the last, we need the full output sequence as input to the next layer. So we need to write it out. For the last layer, we don't need the output sequence. But if we launch one kernel per layer, the output sequence for that layer must be written to global memory if there's another layer. Actually, we could fuse multiple layers into one kernel if we can keep intermediate data in shared memory. But with seq=512 and hidden=256 and batch=10, the intermediate output of one layer is 10*512*256*4 = 5MB, which is way too big for shared memory. So we definitely need to write intermediate outputs to global memory. Given all this, a naive sequential kernel per layer would be: 1. Load x into global memory 2. Load h0, c0 into registers 3. For t in 0..511: a. Compute gates using some parallelization b. Update h, c c. Write output[t] = h 4. Write h_n, c_n The parallelization for step (a) could be: - Each block handles some batch elements and some gates - Or each block handles all batch elements and some gates, then we need a second kernel or synchronization to compute h, c For the gates, we need all 1024 gate values for a batch element before we can compute h and c. So for a fixed batch element b and timestep t: gates[b, :] = x_t[b, :] @ W_ih^T + h_{t-1}[b, :] @ W_hh^T + bias This is essentially two matrix-vector multiplications: (128) @ (128, 1024) and (256) @ (256, 1024). For one batch element, we can use 1024 threads, each computing one gate output. The threads load x_t collaboratively into shared memory and h_{t-1} collaboratively, then each loads its own W_ih row and W_hh row from global memory. But loading one row per thread from global memory means 1024 separate loads, each of 128 or 256 floats. With coalesced memory access, if we organize W_ih as (1024, 128), then thread j loads W_ih[j, :]. This is not coalesced because thread j and j+1 are accessing different rows. Coalesced access would be if threads read adjacent elements from the same row. For example, to read W_ih[j, k], threads with the same j but different k would read adjacent memory. But we want each thread to read an entire row. Actually, if we transpose W_ih to (128, 1024), then column j contains W_ih[:, j]. To compute gate j, we need dot(x_t, W_ih[:, j]). If W_ih is stored as (input_size, 4*hidden_size) in column-major or if we store it transposed as (4*hidden_size, input_size), then thread j can read W_ih[j, k] and adjacent threads read W_ih[j+1, k], which is coalesced. Actually, let me be careful. W_ih in PyTorch nn.LSTM has shape (4*hidden_size, input_size). So W_ih[j, :] is the j-th row. To have coalesced access when each thread handles a different gate j, we'd want threads to read elements within their own row, but from adjacent columns. If threads j and j+1 both read column k of their respective rows, they're reading W_ih[j, k] and W_ih[j+1, k]. In row-major storage, these are separated by input_size elements, so not adjacent. But in column-major storage, they would be adjacent. PyTorch uses row-major. So for coalesced access, it's better to have each thread read adjacent elements within its row. For example, we can have 32 threads per gate, and each warp reads a row in a coalesced manner. But then we need 1024 * 32 = 32768 threads for one batch element, which is a lot. Alternatively, we can have 256 threads per block, each handling 4 gates. Wait, 1024 / 256 = 4. Each thread handles 4 gates. For a fixed batch element b: 1. Load x_t[b, :] into shared memory (128 floats) 2. Load h_{t-1}[b, :] into shared memory (256 floats) 3. Each thread tid handles gate indices tid, tid+256, tid+512, tid+768 4. For each assigned gate j: a. Read W_ih[j, :] from global memory. With 256 threads reading different rows, memory access is strided by input_size. Not great. b. Compute dot product with shared memory x_t c. Read W_hh[j, :] and compute dot product with shared memory h To improve memory access for W, we can process the weights in tiles. For example, load W_ih[:, k:k+32] into shared memory (1024, 32) = 128KB. Then all threads iterate over the 32 columns and compute partial dot products. With 256 threads, we could have them load different parts of W. But a simpler approach: - Load x_t into SMEM - Divide W_ih into column tiles of size, say, 32. - Load W_ih[:, tile_start:tile_end] into SMEM (1024 * 32 = 32K floats = 128KB) - Each thread computes partial dot products for its assigned gates - Iterate over tiles This is much better for memory access because W is loaded in tiles that can be read coalesced. For W_ih of shape (1024, 128), if we load a column tile of size 32, we read W_ih[:, 0:32]. In row-major, this is stored as [W[0,0], W[0,1], ..., W[0,127], W[1,0], ...]. So W[:, 0:32] means reading the first 32 elements of each row. Threads can read these coalesced. Actually, if we transpose W_ih to (128, 1024), then W[j, :] for a fixed j is the j-th row. For column tiles, we'd read rows j:j+tile_size and all columns, which is also coalesced. Let me think about this more carefully. PyTorch LSTM stores W_ih as (4*hidden, input_size). PyTorch is row-major. The element at [i, j] is at memory offset i * input_size + j. If I want threads to read W[:, tile_start:tile_end], that's elements [i, tile_start] through [i, tile_end-1] for all i. Thread 0 reads [0, tile_start..tile_end], thread 1 reads [1, tile_start..tile_end], etc. These are stride-input_size apart, so not coalesced. If I instead store W_ih as (input_size, 4*hidden_size) - i.e., transposed - then W[j, i] is the old W[i, j]. Thread i reading [0, i], [1, i], etc. But we want thread i to read the weights for gate i. For gate i, we need old_W[i, :] = new_W[:, i]. So if new_W is (input_size, 4*hidden), column i is stored contiguously in row-major. Thread i and i+1 read columns i and i+1, which are offset by input_size. Still not coalesced for reading the full column. Wait, to read a full column (input_size elements), we can have multiple threads reading different parts of the same column. For example, 32 threads read elements 0..31 of column i. Then threads 0 and 1 (for columns i and i+1) are reading elements that are input_size apart - not coalesced. Hmm. Actually for a matrix of shape (input_size, 4*hidden) in row-major, the element at [r, c] is at offset r * (4*hidden) + c. If 32 threads read [0, c], [0, c+1], ..., [0, c+31], these are at offsets c, c+1, ..., c+31. These ARE coalesced! So if new_W has shape (input_size, 4*hidden), and we want thread c to compute dot(old_x, old_W[c, :]) = dot(old_x, new_W[:, c]), we can have each thread c read new_W[:, c] one element at a time, and the access pattern is coalesced because at row r, threads read new_W[r, c], new_W[r, c+1], etc., which are contiguous! Wait, that's a transposed view! If I transpose W_ih from (4*hidden, input_size) to (input_size, 4*hidden), then column c of the transposed matrix is row c of the original. And threads reading consecutive columns of the transposed matrix access contiguous memory! So the strategy is: 1. Transpose W_ih and W_hh so they are (input_size, 4*hidden) and (hidden_size, 4*hidden) 2. Threads are indexed by gate id c in range [0, 4*hidden) 3. Each thread c computes: dot(x, transposed_W_ih[:, c]) + dot(h, transposed_W_hh[:, c]) + bias[c] 4. At each row r of the transposed matrix, threads c read element [r, c], which is coalesced! This is beautiful! Let me verify: - Original W_ih: shape (1024, 128), row-major. Row i is at offset i*128. - Transposed W_ih: shape (128, 1024), row-major. Element [r, c] is at offset r*1024 + c. - Threads c=0,1,2,... read [r, 0], [r, 1], [r, 2], ... which are at offsets r*1024 + 0, r*1024 + 1, etc. These are contiguous! Coalesced! Yes! So if we transpose the weights during initialization, we can get fully coalesced access. Now, for the kernel: - Block: (block_size, 1, 1) where block_size = 1024 (number of gates) - Grid: (batch_size, 1, 1) or maybe just 1 block if we want one block per batch element. But with batch=10, we could have 10 blocks. - Actually, a block of 1024 threads is the maximum. If batch=10, we can have 10 blocks of 1024 threads each, processing all batch elements in parallel. For each block b (batch element): 1. Load x_t[b, :] into shared memory? No, x_t[b, :] is 128 floats. With 1024 threads, only first 128 threads participate. But actually, if W_ih is transposed to (128, 1024), then computing dot(x_t[b, :], W_ih[:, c]) means: ``` sum_r x_t[b, r] * W_ih[r, c] ``` For thread c, this iterates over r and reads W_ih[r, c]. The accesses are coalesced (as shown above). But x_t[b, r] is broadcast to all threads. We could load x_t[b, :] into shared memory, but with only 128 floats, it's small. Alternatively, we can just read x_t[b, r] from global memory - since it's broadcast, it'll be cached in L1. 2. Similarly, h_{t-1}[b, :] is 256 floats. We can load it into shared memory or just read from global memory with broadcasting. So each block b does: ``` gates[b, c] = dot(x_t[b, :], W_ih_transposed[:, c]) + dot(h_prev[b, :], W_hh_transposed[:, c]) + bias[c] ``` Then after computing all gates: ``` i = sigmoid(gates[:, 0*H:1*H]) f = sigmoid(gates[:, 1*H:2*H]) g = tanh(gates[:, 2*H:3*H]) o = sigmoid(gates[:, 3*H:4*H]) c_new = f * c_prev + i * g h_new = o * tanh(c_new) ``` For a block of 1024 threads, each thread handles one gate element. So thread c in [0, H-1] handles i[c], thread c in [H, 2H-1] handles f[c-H], etc. After computing gates, each thread c in [0, H-1] updates c_new[c] and h_new[c]. This requires no synchronization within the block except at the very end when writing h_new and c_new back to global memory. Now, can we fit this into one kernel that loops over timesteps? Yes! We launch a kernel with grid=(batch_size, 1, 1), block=(4*hidden_size, 1, 1). For each batch element b: 1. Load h0 and c0 from global memory (or from the appropriate layer's initial state) 2. For t in 0..seq_len-1: a. Read x_t[b, :] from global memory b. Compute gates using transposed W_ih and W_hh c. Apply activations and compute new h, c d. Write output[t, b, :] = h (if needed) e. Update h_prev = h, c_prev = c 3. Write final h and c For H=256, block size = 1024 threads. batch=10 means 10 blocks. For H=256 and input=128 (layer 1): W_ih_transposed is (128, 1024). For each timestep and each block: - Each of 1024 threads reads 128 elements from W_ih_transposed and 256 elements from W_hh_transposed - Plus reads 128 elements from x and 256 elements from h - Computes 1 gate value Total reads per block per timestep: - From W_ih: 128 * 1024 * 4 = 512KB - From W_hh: 256 * 1024 * 4 = 1MB - From x: 128 * 4 = 512 bytes (broadcast, cached) - From h: 256 * 4 = 1KB (broadcast, cached) Wait, but all 10 blocks read the SAME W_ih and W_hh. So total W reads per timestep = 1.5MB. For 512 timesteps: ~768MB of W reads. But for layer 1, W_ih is only 0.5MB and W_hh is 1MB. With L2 cache (40MB on H100), the weights should mostly stay in cache. So the effective bandwidth is much lower. For one layer, we launch one kernel. The kernel loops over 512 timesteps internally. This avoids kernel launch overhead. Now, what about the output? For layers 1-5, we need to write the full output sequence. That's 512 * 10 * 256 * 4 = 5MB per layer. We can write it as we go. For the last layer (layer 6), we don't need to write the output sequence. This kernel design is reasonable. But will it be faster than cuDNN? cuDNN uses highly optimized GEMMs with cublasLt or similar, which can batch operations across timesteps, use tensor cores, etc. For batch=10, hidden=256, cuDNN likely groups multiple timesteps and uses cublas. Our kernel doesn't use tensor cores and does simple dot products. However, our kernel avoids: 1. Writing output for the last layer 2. Separate kernel launches between timesteps 3. Some overhead from PyTorch Python But cuDNN is extremely sophisticated. For this to win, cuDNN must be suffering from some inefficiency for this specific size. Actually, let me think about whether I can make the kernel more efficient. One key optimization: process multiple timesteps per block without writing intermediate outputs to global memory? No, because each timestep depends on the previous h and c. Another optimization: use warp-level primitives for reductions within the dot product? Actually, in our kernel, each thread computes its own gate value independently. There's no reduction needed because each thread handles one output element. The "reduction" is the dot product within each thread. For thread c computing dot(x, W[:, c]): - x has 128 elements - W[:, c] has 128 elements - Thread c reads x[r] and W[r, c] for r = 0 to 127 and accumulates - This takes 128 iterations In each iteration r, thread c reads W[r, c]. As we established, for fixed r and varying c, accesses are coalesced. But for consecutive r, thread c reads W[r, c] and then W[r+1, c], which are stride 1024 apart. Not coalesced across iterations! So within one thread, the memory access pattern is: read W[0, c], W[1, c], W[2, c], ... which are at offsets c, 1024+c, 2048+c, etc. This is a stride-1024 access pattern for each thread, which is very bad for cache! We read one float from each cache line. To fix this, we can load W into shared memory in a transposed manner, or load tiles that contain W[r:r+tile, c_start:c_end]. Actually, for W of shape (128, 1024), we can load it in row tiles. Say tile_size = 32 rows. We load W[r:r+32, :] into shared memory. That's 32 * 1024 floats = 128KB. Then each thread can read its column c from the shared memory tile. This is much better! We load data coalesced (all threads read adjacent elements in row r), store in shared memory, then each thread reads its own element from shared memory (no coalescing issue for SMEM). Yes! This is the standard tiling approach for GEMM. So refined kernel for one layer: - Block size: (1024, 1, 1) - one thread per gate - Each block handles one batch element: grid=(batch_size, 1, 1) - Shared memory: input_tile (32 floats) + W_ih_tile (32 x 1024 floats) + W_hh_tile (32 x 1024 floats) + h_prev (256 floats) + c_prev (256 floats) + x_t (128 floats) Wait, W_ih_tile of 32 x 1024 x 4 bytes = 131KB W_hh_tile of 32 x 1024 x 4 bytes = 131KB h_prev = 1KB c_prev = 1KB x_t = 0.5KB Total: ~264KB + some overhead. This is close to the 227KB limit per block on H100! Actually, H100 shared memory per block can go up to 227KB. 264KB > 227KB, so we can't have both W_ih and W_hh tiles of 32K floats simultaneously. Options: 1. Reduce tile size: 16 rows for each W. Then 16*1024*4 = 64KB each. Total: 128KB + 2.5KB = 130.5KB. Fits! 2. Or don't load W_hh into shared memory simultaneously. Compute W_ih part first, store partial sum. Then compute W_hh part. For option 1 with tile_size=16 for rows: - W_ih is (128, 1024), so 8 tiles of 16 rows each - W_hh is (256, 1024), so 16 tiles of 16 rows each - Total shared memory: 2 * 64KB + 2.5KB = 130.5KB. Fits easily! Actually, we don't need W_ih and W_hh simultaneously. We can reuse the shared memory: 1. Load W_ih tile into SMEM 2. All threads compute partial dot products 3. Load W_hh tile into SMEM (overwriting W_ih tile) 4. All threads add partial dot products 5. Add bias, compute activations This reduces SMEM to 64KB + 2.5KB = 66.5KB. Very comfortable. Let me detail the algorithm: ``` // SMEM layout: // float smem_W[16][1024]; // 64KB // float smem_input[16]; // shared with smem_W, or separate small buffer // float smem_h[256]; // 1KB // float smem_c[256]; // 1KB // float smem_x[128]; // 0.5KB // But we can reuse smem_W for both W_ih and W_hh. shared float smem_W[16][1024]; shared float smem_state[512]; // For h and c shared float smem_input[128]; // For x // Initialize h and c from h0, c0 for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < hidden_size; i += blockDim.x) { smem_state[i] = h0[blockIdx.x, i]; smem_state[hidden_size + i] = c0[blockIdx.x, i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Load x_t for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < input_size; i += blockDim.x) { smem_input[i] = x[t, blockIdx.x, i]; } __syncthreads(); float gate_val = 0.0f; // Process W_ih in tiles for (int tile = 0; tile < input_size / 16; tile++) { // Load W_ih tile: rows [tile*16, tile*16+15], all columns for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 16 * 1024; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / 1024; int col = i % 1024; smem_W[row][col] = W_ih_transposed[tile*16 + row, col]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes partial dot product for its gate int gate_idx = threadIdx.x; float partial = 0.0f; for (int r = 0; r < 16; r++) { partial += smem_input[tile*16 + r] * smem_W[r][gate_idx]; } gate_val += partial; __syncthreads(); } // Process W_hh in tiles for (int tile = 0; tile < hidden_size / 16; tile++) { // Load W_hh tile for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 16 * 1024; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / 1024; int col = i % 1024; smem_W[row][col] = W_hh_transposed[tile*16 + row, col]; } __syncthreads(); int gate_idx = threadIdx.x; float partial = 0.0f; for (int r = 0; r < 16; r++) { partial += smem_state[tile*16 + r] * smem_W[r][gate_idx]; } gate_val += partial; __syncthreads(); } // Add bias gate_val += bias[threadIdx.x]; // Apply activations int gate_idx = threadIdx.x; float i_gate, f_gate, g_gate, o_gate; if (gate_idx < hidden_size) { i_gate = sigmoid(gate_val); } else if (gate_idx < 2*hidden_size) { f_gate = sigmoid(gate_val); } else if (gate_idx < 3*hidden_size) { g_gate = tanhf(gate_val); } else { o_gate = sigmoid(gate_val); } __syncthreads(); // Wait for all gates to be computed // Update c and h if (gate_idx < hidden_size) { float c_prev = smem_state[hidden_size + gate_idx]; float c_new = f_gate * c_prev + i_gate * g_gate; float h_new = o_gate * tanhf(c_new); smem_state[hidden_size + gate_idx] = c_new; smem_state[gate_idx] = h_new; // Write output if (output != nullptr) { output[t, blockIdx.x, gate_idx] = h_new; } } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, there's a problem. The `__syncthreads()` before applying activations assumes all threads have computed their gate_val. But `gate_val` is a local variable, not in shared memory. The `__syncthreads()` ensures all threads have reached this point, which is fine. Then each thread only updates one element of c and h based on its gate index. But threads with gate_idx >= hidden_size don't participate in the state update. That's fine - they just idle. But we need all gates to be computed before updating c and h. The first `__syncthreads()` after computing gate_val handles that. Then after c and h update, we need another `__syncthreads()` before the next timestep so that all threads see the updated state. There's an issue with the activation computation: each thread computes one gate value, but to compute c_new and h_new, a thread needs i_gate[gate_idx], f_gate[gate_idx], g_gate[gate_idx], o_gate[gate_idx]. These are computed by 4 different threads: thread gate_idx, gate_idx+H, gate_idx+2H, gate_idx+3H. So thread gate_idx needs to read the gate values from the other threads. How can it do that? We can store all gate values in shared memory! Add: `shared float smem_gates[4 * hidden_size]; // 4KB` Then after computing gate_val, each thread writes to `smem_gates[threadIdx.x] = gate_val;` and then `__syncthreads();` Then all threads can read the four gates they need: ``` float i = sigmoid(smem_gates[gate_idx]); float f = sigmoid(smem_gates[gate_idx + H]); float g = tanhf(smem_gates[gate_idx + 2*H]); float o = sigmoid(smem_gates[gate_idx + 3*H]); ``` This is much cleaner. Let me revise: ```cpp __shared__ float smem_W[16][1024]; __shared__ float smem_state[512]; // h and c __shared__ float smem_input[128]; // x __shared__ float smem_gates[1024]; // gate values // Initialize h and c for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < hidden_size; i += blockDim.x) { smem_state[i] = h0[blockIdx.x * hidden_size + i]; smem_state[hidden_size + i] = c0[blockIdx.x * hidden_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; t++) { // Load x_t for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < input_size; i += blockDim.x) { smem_input[i] = x[t * batch_size * input_size + blockIdx.x * input_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); float gate_val = 0.0f; // W_ih for (int tile = 0; tile < (input_size + 15) / 16; tile++) { int tile_rows = min(16, input_size - tile * 16); // Load tile for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < tile_rows * 1024; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / 1024; int col = i % 1024; smem_W[row][col] = W_ih_transposed[(tile * 16 + row) * 1024 + col]; } __syncthreads(); int gate_idx = threadIdx.x; float partial = 0.0f; for (int r = 0; r < tile_rows; r++) { partial += smem_input[tile * 16 + r] * smem_W[r][gate_idx]; } gate_val += partial; __syncthreads(); } // W_hh for (int tile = 0; tile < (hidden_size + 15) / 16; tile++) { int tile_rows = min(16, hidden_size - tile * 16); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < tile_rows * 1024; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / 1024; int col = i % 1024; smem_W[row][col] = W_hh_transposed[(tile * 16 + row) * 1024 + col]; } __syncthreads(); int gate_idx = threadIdx.x; float partial = 0.0f; for (int r = 0; r < tile_rows; r++) { partial += smem_state[tile * 16 + r] * smem_W[r][gate_idx]; } gate_val += partial; __syncthreads(); } // Add bias gate_val += bias[threadIdx.x]; smem_gates[threadIdx.x] = gate_val; __syncthreads(); // Update c and h int h_idx = threadIdx.x; if (h_idx < hidden_size) { float i_gate = sigmoid(smem_gates[h_idx]); float f_gate = sigmoid(smem_gates[h_idx + hidden_size]); float g_gate = tanhf(smem_gates[h_idx + 2 * hidden_size]); float o_gate = sigmoid(smem_gates[h_idx + 3 * hidden_size]); float c_prev = smem_state[hidden_size + h_idx]; float c_new = f_gate * c_prev + i_gate * g_gate; float h_new = o_gate * tanhf(c_new); smem_state[hidden_size + h_idx] = c_new; smem_state[h_idx] = h_new; if (output != nullptr) { output[t * batch_size * hidden_size + blockIdx.x * hidden_size + h_idx] = h_new; } } __syncthreads(); } ``` This looks correct and reasonably efficient. However, there's a subtle issue: for each thread, the inner loop `for (int r = 0; r < tile_rows; r++) { partial += smem_input[...] * smem_W[r][gate_idx]; }` involves reading `smem_W[r][gate_idx]` where `gate_idx` is `threadIdx.x`. Since all threads read different columns but the same row within the loop, and shared memory is banked, threads with `threadIdx.x` values that differ by 32 will be in the same bank group... Actually, for 32-bit floats, shared memory has 32 banks. Thread c reading `smem_W[r][c]` means offset c within the row. If the row has 1024 elements, then consecutive threads (0, 1, 2, ...) read elements (0, 1, 2, ...) in the same row. These map to banks (0, 1, 2, ...), so no bank conflicts. But when we advance to the next row `r+1`, thread c reads `smem_W[r+1][c]`, which is offset ` (r+1)*1024 + c`. Since 1024 is a multiple of 32, the bank is still `c % 32`. So again, no bank conflicts. Wait, but `smem_W` is declared as `float smem_W[16][1024]`. In C/CUDA, this is stored row-major. `smem_W[r][c]` is at offset `r * 1024 + c`. Banks are `offset % 32 = c % 32`. So thread c accesses bank `c % 32`. Consecutive threads access consecutive banks, no conflicts. But we have 1024 threads. Warp 0: threads 0-31. Warp 1: threads 32-63. Both warps access banks 0-31 simultaneously. Since H100 has broadcast capability and the shared memory bandwidth is high, this might be fine with no conflicts (each bank can serve one request per warp, I think). Actually, for threads within a warp, bank conflicts occur if multiple threads in the SAME warp access the same bank. But here, threads 0-31 access banks 0-31 (one per bank), threads 32-63 also access banks 0-31 (one per bank), and these are in DIFFERENT warps. Different warps don't have bank conflicts. So no shared memory bank conflicts! Now, the global memory access for loading W tiles: `W_ih_transposed[(tile * 16 + row) * 1024 + col]` For a fixed row and varying col (with varying threadIdx.x), accesses are contiguous. Coalesced. For loading x_t: `x[t * batch_size * input_size + blockIdx.x * input_size + i]` Thread i reads x[... + i]. Contiguous. Coalesced. For writing output: `output[t * batch_size * hidden_size + blockIdx.x * hidden_size + h_idx]` Thread h_idx writes to contiguous locations. Coalesced. This kernel has very good memory access patterns. Now, what's the compute intensity? For layer 1 with input=128, hidden=256, gates=1024, batch=10, seq=512: - Per timestep per batch element: - W_ih dot: 128 * 1024 = 131K FLOPs - W_hh dot: 256 * 1024 = 262K FLOPs - Activations: ~4K FLOPs - Total: ~393K FLOPs - Per layer: 393K * 512 * 10 = ~2.01 GFLOPs (layer 1) - Layers 2-6: 524K * 512 * 10 = ~2.68 GFLOPs each, total 13.4 GFLOPs - Total: ~15.4 GFLOPs All these FLOPs happen within the kernel without writing intermediate gate matrices to global memory. The only global memory writes are for layer outputs (25MB total for layers 1-5) and the final states. Global memory reads: inputs (2.5MB), weights (~9MB, cached), outputs of previous layers for next layers (25MB read). Total memory traffic: ~61.5MB. With 3.35 TB/s, minimum time for memory is 18.4 µs. Compute time at 67 TFLOPS is 230 µs. So this is compute bound. Can we achieve high compute utilization? Each thread does 128+256=384 multiply-adds per timestep. With 1024 threads per block and 10 blocks, that's 10,240 threads. Each does 384 FMAs. Plus the memory loads from shared memory. The FMA loop is bounded by the shared memory latency. But with enough unrolling and instruction-level parallelism, we should get decent throughput. However, I realize there's a potential issue: the kernel has a very long runtime because it loops over 512 timesteps. On H100, there's a maximum kernel runtime (watchdog timer), but for compute kernels it's usually fine as long as it's under a few seconds. 512 iterations with 384 FMAs each should be microseconds to milliseconds, so fine. Another issue: I need to handle variable input_size and hidden_size. In the constructor, input_size=128, hidden_size=256, but the kernel should be generic. Also, nn.LSTM has two sets of biases: bias_ih and bias_hh. In the reference, `has_biases=True` by default. So the total bias is bias_ih + bias_hh. For weight storage: PyTorch stores W_ih as (4*hidden_size, input_size). I need to transpose it to (input_size, 4*hidden_size) for the kernel. I can do this in `__init__`. Similarly for W_hh: (4*hidden_size, hidden_size) -> transpose to (hidden_size, 4*hidden_size). For biases, I can pre-add bias_ih and bias_hh into a single bias of size (4*hidden_size,). Now, for multiple layers: I'll launch the kernel 6 times. The output of layer k becomes the input of layer k+1. For layer k, the input size might differ from the hidden size. Layers 2-6 have input_size = hidden_size. Also, h0 and c0 have shape (num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size). For layer k, I use h0[k] and c0[k]. After all layers, I need to return h_n for all layers. The kernel writes the final h and c for each layer. I can launch one kernel per layer and collect the final h and c states. Actually, the kernel needs to return both h_n and c_n. I also need to handle storing the output sequence for intermediate layers. Let me think about the host-side wrapper. It should: 1. For each layer: a. Get the appropriate weights b. Launch the kernel with x (input sequence), h0[k], c0[k], W_ih_t, W_hh_t, bias c. Kernel computes and returns output sequence and final h, c d. x = output sequence for next layer 2. Stack all final h's into a (num_layers, batch, hidden) tensor 3. Return the h tensor Wait, the kernel writes the output to global memory. So I need to pre-allocate the output tensor and pass it to the kernel. For the last layer, output can be None. For the final state, I need to allocate tensors for h_n and c_n and have the kernel write to them at the end. One optimization: for intermediate layers, the output can be written in-place over the input buffer if we don't need the input anymore. But since we process layer by layer, after layer k finishes, we don't need its input anymore. So we can reuse the same tensor for alternating input/output. But careful with the shapes: layer 1 output is (batch, seq, hidden). Layer 2 input is also (batch, seq, hidden). So we can use two buffers and swap them. But the PyTorch LSTM returns the output of the LAST layer as its `out`. For our case, we don't need `out` at all! We only need `h_n` from each layer. Wait, but `state[0]` is the final hidden state of ALL layers. For layer k, we need its final h and c which are `h0[k]` input to the next... no, actually `h_n` is the state after processing the sequence. So for layers 1-6, I need to process each layer sequentially, and each layer needs the full output of the previous layer as its input (except layer 1 which takes x). But wait - I only need `h_n`, the final hidden state. I don't need to return `out`. So for layers 2-6, I DO need the full output of the previous layer as input. So I must materialize the inter-layer outputs. So my host wrapper: ```python def lstm_custom_forward(x, h0, c0, weights): # x: (batch, seq, input_size) # h0: (num_layers, batch, hidden_size) # c0: (num_layers, batch, hidden_size) # weights: list of (W_ih_t, W_hh_t, bias) for each layer batch_size, seq_len, _ = x.shape num_layers = len(weights) hidden_size = h0.shape[2] # Pre-allocate two buffers for alternating layers buffer1 = torch.empty(batch_size, seq_len, hidden_size, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) buffer2 = torch.empty(batch_size, seq_len, hidden_size, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) # Final states h_n = torch.empty_like(h0) c_n = torch.empty_like(c0) current_input = x for layer_idx in range(num_layers): W_ih_t, W_hh_t, bias = weights[layer_idx] is_last_layer = (layer_idx == num_layers - 1) # Choose output buffer if is_last_layer: output_ptr = None # Kernel doesn't write output else: output_ptr = buffer1 if layer_idx % 2 == 0 else buffer2 # Launch kernel launch_lstm_kernel( current_input, h0[layer_idx], c0[layer_idx], W_ih_t, W_hh_t, bias, output_ptr, h_n[layer_idx], c_n[layer_idx] ) current_input = output_ptr return h_n ``` Actually, for the last layer, we don't need output. But for layers 1-5, we do. So we can just not allocate an output buffer for layer 6. But in the C++ wrapper, "output_ptr = None" needs to be handled. We can pass a null pointer or a boolean flag. Actually, passing a boolean or checking if the tensor is defined is easy in C++: ```cpp if (output.has_value()) { // write output } ``` But simpler: for the last layer, pass an empty tensor and check `output.numel() > 0`. Now, let me calculate the expected performance of this kernel. For each block (batch element), the kernel loops over 512 timesteps. At each timestep, each thread does: - ~384 FMAs (128 for W_ih + 256 for W_hh) - Several shared memory loads - Some global memory loads (weight tiles) For 1024 threads and 10 blocks, total parallelism is 10,240 threads. The weight tiles are loaded by all threads. Each tile load is coalesced. But there are many tile loads: - Layer 1: input_size=128, so 128/16 = 8 tiles for W_ih. hidden_size=256, so 256/16 = 16 tiles for W_hh. Total 24 tiles per timestep. - For 512 timesteps: 12,288 tile loads. Each tile is 16*1024*4 = 64KB. Total: ~786MB of weight reads. - But weights are only 1.5MB total, and with 40MB L2 cache, they stay cached. So effective DRAM bandwidth for weights is just the initial load into L2. After that, it's L2 -> SMEM. So the memory traffic from global memory is primarily: 1. Reading x for layer 1: 2.5MB 2. Writing layer 1 output and reading it for layer 2: 5MB write + 5MB read 3. ... same for layers 2-5 4. Reading x for layers 2-6 (which is the previous layer's output) So total global memory traffic is roughly 2.5 + 5*5*2 = 52.5MB for the sequence data plus 1.5MB for weights. At 3.35 TB/s, 52.5MB takes ~15.6 µs. The compute is 15.4 GFLOPs, which at peak 67 TFLOPS takes 230 µs. But for small tile GEMMs, we won't get peak FLOPS. Wait, actually each thread is doing independent FMAs with smem operands. The throughput of FMA with smem operands is typically close to peak on modern GPUs. Let me estimate: Each thread does 384 FMAs per timestep. 512 timesteps = 196,608 FMAs per thread. 10,240 threads = ~2B FMAs total per layer? Wait no, that can't be right. Actually: 128 FMAs for W_ih dot + 256 FMAs for W_hh dot = 384 FMAs per thread per timestep. For batch=10: 3,840 FMAs per timestep. For seq=512: ~1.97M FMAs per layer 1. We avoid writing output for the last layer (saves 5MB) 2. We don't compute the FC layer 3. Our kernel is specifically tailored to this small batch size But actually, for LSTM, cuDNN might be using optimized GEMM implementations that achieve much higher FLOPS than our manual kernel. For example, cublasLt might use tensor cores and achieve hundreds of TFLOPS for batched GEMMs. Even though batch=10 is small, cuDNN might batch across timesteps. Let me reconsider whether my kernel can actually beat cuDNN. For small batch sizes, I found in some benchmarks that custom kernels CAN beat cuDNN because cuDNN's GEMM approach has overhead. Also, my kernel computes the LSTM in a single kernel launch per layer, avoiding the overhead of separate GEMM + bias + activation kernels. I think this is worth a try. Let me also consider additional optimizations: 1. Unroll the tile loops by constant factors where possible 2. Use `__ldg` for read-only data 3. Process multiple batch elements per block to improve occupancy and hide latency Actually, occupancy might be an issue. With 1024 threads per block and 227KB shared memory per block, how many blocks can run per SM? H100 has 64K registers per SM. With 1024 threads, if each uses ~32 registers, that's 32K registers. Plus shared memory of ~70KB. So probably 1 block per SM at a time. With 132 SMs on H100 SXM, we can run 132 blocks concurrently. But we only have 10 blocks! So only 10 SMs are used, leaving 122 SMs idle. This is bad! We have terrible occupancy. Only 10 blocks total means we can't even fill one wave of the GPU. So we can have a kernel that processes a chunk of timesteps: ```cpp __global__ void lstm_chunk_kernel( const float* input, const float* h_init, const float* c_init, const float* W_ih, const float* W_hh, const float* bias, float* output, float* h_final, float* c_final, int batch_size, int seq_len, int input_size, int hidden_size, int chunk_start, int chunk_size ) { // Each block handles one batch element int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= batch_size) return; // Load h_init and c_init // Process timesteps from chunk_start to chunk_start + chunk_size // Write h_final and c_final } ``` Then host code: ```cpp torch::Tensor h = h0[layer].clone(); torch::Tensor c = c0[layer].clone(); for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; chunk++) { lstm_chunk_kernel<<<batch_size, 1024, shared_mem>>>( input, h.data_ptr(), c.data_ptr(), ..., output, h.data_ptr(), c.data_ptr(), chunk * chunk_size, chunk_size ); } ``` But wait, `h` and `c` are overwritten in-place. If we launch kernels asynchronously, the next kernel might read h before the previous one finishes writing it. We need to synchronize between chunks. ```cpp for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; chunk++) { lstm_chunk_kernel<<<batch_size, 1024, shared_mem, stream>>>(...); cudaStreamSynchronize(stream); // Wait for chunk to finish } ``` This adds kernel launch overhead per chunk. For 8 chunks, that's 8 launches per layer, 48 launches total. Each launch has ~5-10 microseconds of overhead. So 48 * 10 = 480 microseconds of overhead. This might negate the benefits. Alternatively, we can use a single kernel with all blocks processing their chunk, and use `__threadfence()` and polling to synchronize between chunks. But that's complex and error-prone.
